Title: Stage B Debugging
ID: 69cfa4b5-0524-838c-ae26-f29bb6692759

User: The uploaded software running in a conda environment is made to correct DeepLabCut extracted body part assignments to two individual mice with help of 3D reconstruction using .cpp-coded Ceres bundle adjustment with a python wrapper. Stage A concerned with 3D reconstruction of individual points seems to work reasonable. In stage B segmentation .png files with 0 for background, 1 for mouse0 and 2 for mouse 1 are used to hypothesize improved mouse ids for body parts that are better than the DLC-based ids. Subsequently to the segmentation based ids that could be inconsistent, the 3D reconstruction results are use to "homogenize" the id decisions. So far stage B does not work properly.

Please add with a CLI option --debug_stageB detailed screen test prints. Start with frame ID that is processed. Report the results concerning the segmentation evaluation around the image points of the body parts in that fractions of background, mouse0 and mouse1 ratios/weights are reported. Continue with information how the homogenized decision is made. Do not change anything in the procedure. Only add debug prints. Do not remove anything that is in the program at this point.

Please summarize here, how the stage B algorithm works. If you have comments regarding what may currently may mess up the id assignments (it is worse than DLC assignments currently), please let me know in the chat. But do not change the code at this point in time. We want to inspect the system first and then decide about changes.

### How to run
Just add the new flag:
```bash
python run_mouse3d.py \
  --cameras .../cameras.json \
  --dlc .../dlc_long.csv \
  --seg-dir .../segmentation_dir \
  --out .../tuneB \
  --debug_stageB
```
Tip: combine with `--frames START END` to keep output manageable.

---

## Stage B algorithm summary (as implemented)
Stage B takes Stage A’s per-bodypart hypotheses (0–2 triangulated 3D points + leftover single 2D detections) and tries to produce consistent mouse identities using segmentation + 3D continuity:

1. **B1 Segmentation evidence per 2D observation**
   - For each camera, load the integer mask for this frame (expected labels: `0=bg, 1=mouse0, 2=mouse1`). fileciteturn1file2
   - For each 2D observation `uv`, compute **Gaussian-weighted label fractions** `(w_bg,w_m0,w_m1)` in a window around `uv`. fileciteturn1file2
   - Convert `(w_m0,w_m1)` into a binary vote `(dv0,dv1)` via `vote_thr`.
   - Sum votes across the observations that formed one triangulated item → `item.vote=(v0,v1)`.
   - If votes are one-sided, mark `item.exclusive = 0 or 1` (exclusive segmentation evidence), else `None`. fileciteturn2file0

2. **B2 Swap-state decision (global “swap” vs “no swap” vs unknown)**
   - Using **anchor bodyparts** (default `("head","tail_root")`), compare current anchors against predicted previous anchor positions under:
     - `NOSWAP` hypothesis
     - `SWAP` hypothesis
   - Costs are primarily squared distances (scaled by `sigma_motion_anchor`) plus small exclusive-based bias.
   - If ambiguous/insufficient, optionally **carry forward** previous swap state for up to `carry_swap_max_gap` frames, else `UNKNOWN`. fileciteturn2file0

3. **B3 Build per-mouse 3D “models”**
   - Assign current anchor points to mouse0/mouse1 according to swap_state and continuity.
   - Build per-mouse:
     - `head`, `tail_root` (with fallback to previous)
     - `centroid` (from head/tail or fallback)
     - `axis_line` (tail→head direction or fallback) fileciteturn2file0

4. **B4 Assign identities to triangulated 3D items**
   - For each bodypart:
     - If there are **two** 3D points, evaluate both pairings (A→0,B→1 vs A→1,B→0) using `assignment_cost` and pick the lower cost.
     - If **one** 3D point: if `exclusive` exists, use it; otherwise pick min cost between mouse 0/1.
   - `assignment_cost` combines:
     - exclusive penalty/bonus
     - 3D continuity (distance to predicted previous point)
     - proximity to mouse centroid
     - proximity to mouse axis line fileciteturn2file0

5. **B5 Build per-camera DLC-id → mouse-id majority map**
   - From resolved (triangulated) items, gather `(dlc_id, mouse_id)` pairs per camera, choose majority mapping for dlc_id 0 and 1. fileciteturn2file0

6. **B6 Assign identities to leftover singles**
   - If exclusive segmentation exists → use it.
   - Else if **no segmentation signal at all** (`vote=(0,0)`), use the camera majority map if available.
   - Else fall back to continuity by projecting previous 3D points into the camera and comparing 2D distances. fileciteturn2file0

Stage B also writes per-observation `seg_w_*` and votes to the assignments output for offline inspection. fileciteturn3file3

---

## Why Stage B might currently make IDs worse than DLC (things to look for with the new debug)
These are the highest-probability failure modes given the current implementation:

1. **Segmentation masks aren’t actually labeled 0/1/2**
   - If your PNGs are e.g. `{0, 255}` or `{0, 1, 2}` in RGB but not in channel 0, then `patch==1` / `patch==2` will never match → votes become `(0,0)` everywhere. fileciteturn1file2turn2file0  
   - Your new debug prints include per-camera `unique=...` label summaries; if you don’t see `0,1,2`, Stage B is effectively running in “segmentation absent” mode even though files exist.

2. **Frame indexing mismatch for segmentation (off-by-one / wrong start index)**
   - `SegmentationDirectorySource` tries to infer filename pattern and start index; if it guesses wrong, you’ll read the **wrong frame’s** mask, which can easily invert exclusives and poison swap + costs.
   - With debug on, check whether weights look plausible (e.g., head points should usually have strong mouse weights, not mostly background).

3. **`vote_thr=0.01` is extremely permissive**
   - With a Gaussian window, even a tiny overlap with the other mouse can exceed 1% → many observations may vote for **both** mice, destroying exclusivity and making segmentation unhelpful (or subtly misleading).
   - Debug will show many `seg_w(... m0≈..., m1≈...)` with both above threshold.

4. **Swap decision can be underconstrained / sticky**
   - If anchors are missing/noisy, `decide_swap_state` can return `UNKNOWN` or carry forward an old swap for up to `carry_swap_max_gap` frames. A wrong swap carried forward will systematically flip identities and can look “worse than DLC”.

5. **Bad 3D points early → bad camera majority maps → cascade**
   - B5 uses *current* triangulated assignments to infer per-camera DLC-id mapping. If early frames are wrong, singles will follow the wrong mapping and reinforce the error.

If you paste a short excerpt of the new Stage-B debug output for a few frames (especially one where it clearly fails), I can help pinpoint which of the above is actually happening—without changing the algorithm yet.

What is the shape 512x512 of the mask?  The video frames have a different size. In B1 the seg_w values seem to prefer background or nothing. votes for mice seem 0 for both mice all the time for that reason.

Assistant: Yes — what you’re seeing is exactly what the current code would produce if the segmentation masks live in a **different pixel coordinate system / resolution** than the DLC observations.

### Why are the masks 512×512?
Because the segmentation loader **does not resize** anything. It reads the PNG “as-is” and returns its native array. In `SegmentationDirectorySource.get_mask()`, it just `imread()`s the file and returns `img.astype(np.int32)` (dropping to channel 0 if RGB). fileciteturn4file0

So `shape=(512,512)` is simply the actual resolution of your saved mask PNGs (very likely produced by the segmentation pipeline/SAM preprocessing).

Meanwhile your cameras are defined as **960×720** in `cameras.json`. fileciteturn5file0  
And DLC `uv` coordinates are in that camera image coordinate system.

### Why do `seg_w` become `(bg=1, m0=0, m1=0)` or `(0,0,0)`?
Stage B computes segmentation weights by sampling a Gaussian window around the **raw DLC uv** in the mask: fileciteturn4file6

`seg_weights_from_mask(mask, uv, kernel)`:
- Rounds `uv` to integer pixel `(u,v)`
- Extracts a patch in the mask around `(u,v)`, **clipped to mask bounds**
- Sums kernel weights where `patch==0/1/2`
- Normalizes to fractions `(w_bg,w_m0,w_m1)` fileciteturn4file0

Two key consequences:

1) **If (u,v) is inside the 512×512 mask but lands on label 0 pixels**, then the kernel mass is almost entirely background ⇒ `w_bg≈1.0`, `w_m0=w_m1≈0.0`. That’s what most of your lines show.

2) **If (u,v) is outside the 512×512 mask**, the clipped slice becomes empty, all sums are 0, and the function returns `(0.0,0.0,0.0)` (because `s<=0`). fileciteturn4file0  
That matches your debug lines where `uv.x` is e.g. `528` (greater than 511), giving `seg_w(bg=0,m0=0,m1=0)`.

So the “votes always (0,0)” are not mysterious: the segmentation window is almost never overlapping mask labels 1 or 2, either because it’s sampling background *or* sampling out-of-bounds.

### The most likely root cause
A **resolution/letterbox mismatch** between:
- DLC coordinates in 960×720 space (camera space) fileciteturn5file0
- Segmentation masks stored in 512×512 space fileciteturn4file0

The label pixel counts you printed (`~1000` pixels per mouse out of `262k`) are actually consistent with a common pipeline: resize 960×720 down to width 512 (scale ≈ 0.533) → becomes 512×384, then pad to 512×512 (64 px top + 64 px bottom). If that’s what happened, then the *correct* mapping from DLC uv to mask uv would include **both scaling and vertical offset**. Right now Stage B applies **neither**, so it samples the wrong locations.

### Why this can make IDs “worse than DLC”
When segmentation provides no signal, Stage B’s early frames have:
- no previous anchors (`swap_state=UNKNOWN`)
- no centroid/axis model built
- continuity costs effectively off

So costs become identical (often 0), and assignments devolve into **tie-breaking by list order** (e.g., A→0, B→1), which can seed `prev_state` incorrectly and then propagate through continuity and camera-majority mapping. (That’s exactly what your frame 0 shows: every `terms={...}` is 0.) fileciteturn4file6

### Quick confirmation test (no code changes to pipeline)
Run this in an interactive python shell on one of the failing points (use any mask path you already know exists):

```python
import numpy as np
import imageio.v3 as iio
from mice3d.segmentation import GaussianKernel, seg_weights_from_mask

mask = iio.imread(".../cam1_top/mask_000000.png")  # adjust path
if mask.ndim == 3:
    mask = mask[:, :, 0]
mask = mask.astype(np.int32)

kernel = GaussianKernel.make(radius=9, sigma=4.0)

uv = np.array([380.729, 326.804])  # your DLC uv example
print("raw:", seg_weights_from_mask(mask, uv, kernel))

# hypothesis A: simple anisotropic resize (960x720 -> 512x512 directly)
uv_a = np.array([uv[0] * (512/960), uv[1] * (512/720)])
print("anisotropic:", uv_a, seg_weights_from_mask(mask, uv_a, kernel))

# hypothesis B: letterbox: scale by 512/960, then pad vertically to 512
s = 512/960
pad_y = (512 - 720*s) / 2.0
uv_b = np.array([uv[0]*s, uv[1]*s + pad_y])
print("letterbox:", uv_b, "pad_y=", pad_y, seg_weights_from_mask(mask, uv_b, kernel))
```

If either `anisotropic` or `letterbox` suddenly yields `w_m0` or `w_m1` noticeably > 0, you’ve confirmed the coordinate mismatch is the cause of the “all background / all zeros” votes.

If you paste **one** example where the “letterbox” transform produces a strong mouse weight (or show that it doesn’t), I can narrow it to: (a) pure scaling, (b) scaling+padding, (c) cropping/ROI offset, or (d) frame misalignment.

raw: (1.0, 0.0, 0.0)
anisotropic: [203.05546667 232.39395556] (0.6575364295735613, 0.34246357042643877, 0.0)
letterbox: [203.05546667 238.29546667] pad_y= 64.0 (0.27461063696051785, 0.7253893630394822, 0.0)

Assistant: That output is the smoking gun.

### What it proves (unambiguously)
Your segmentation masks are in a **letterboxed 512×512 coordinate system**, while DLC points are in **960×720 camera coordinates**.

- **raw (using DLC uv directly in the 512×512 mask)** gives `(1.0,0.0,0.0)` → you’re sampling the wrong location (background). fileciteturn6file0
- **anisotropic scale** (x·512/960, y·512/720) starts to overlap mouse0: `m0≈0.34`. fileciteturn6file0
- **letterbox scale + vertical pad** (scale by 512/960, then +64 px in y) overlaps mouse0 strongly: `m0≈0.73`. fileciteturn6file0

And the derived `pad_y=64` matches exactly:
- scale `s=512/960=0.5333…`
- scaled height `720*s = 384`
- padding to 512 → `(512-384)/2 = 64`

So the masks are almost certainly generated by:
1) resize 960×720 → 512×384 (keep aspect)
2) pad vertically to 512×512 (64 top + 64 bottom)

### Why Stage B currently collapses
Given this mismatch, Stage B’s segmentation sampling mostly:
- hits background → `seg_w(bg≈1)`
- or goes out-of-bounds (e.g., x>511) → `(0,0,0)` from the empty patch case fileciteturn4file0

That yields votes `(0,0)` everywhere, which you observed. With no segmentation exclusives and no previous anchors in frame 0, almost all assignment costs become identical (often 0), so identities devolve into tie-breaking and then propagate via continuity / cam-majority mapping.

### What to do *now* (without changing the code yet)
You’re in the right place: StageB debug is doing its job. Next diagnostic steps that don’t change the algorithm:

1) **Confirm letterbox transform for each camera dir** (top/front/side) and a couple frames.  
   Run your `letterboxCheck.py` (or tiny variant) on a few representative DLC points per camera. fileciteturn6file0  
   You should consistently see the letterbox version produce meaningful `m0/m1` weights.

2) In the StageB debug output, look for any `uv=(...,...)` where x>512 or y>512 — those are guaranteed to produce `(0,0,0)` unless transformed.

Once you’ve confirmed this across cameras, the fix is conceptually straightforward: apply the same letterbox mapping to `uv` before calling `seg_weights_from_mask`. But per your instruction, we won’t touch procedure/code until you decide.

If you paste one more `letterboxCheck` run for **cam2_front** or **cam3_side** (same uv logic but with those masks), I can tell you whether all three cameras share the same resize+pad rule or whether any have additional cropping/offset.

Assistant: Your new 3-camera check makes it very clear what’s going on:

### 1) The 512×512 mask is a **letterboxed** view of a 960×720 frame
Across all three cams you used:
- scale `s = 512/960 = 0.53333…`
- vertical padding `pad_y = (512 - 720*s)/2 = 64`
- `pad_x = 0`

…and with that mapping, segmentation weights become strongly mouse-specific (often near 1.0), whereas `raw` sampling is almost always background. That matches your printout exactly. fileciteturn7file1

### 2) Why Stage B currently outputs mostly background / zeros
Stage B calls segmentation like this:

- It reads the mask “as-is” (no resizing), returning its native array (512×512 here). fileciteturn7file6
- Then it samples the mask at the **original DLC uv** coordinates: `seg_weights_from_mask(mask, obs.uv, kernel)`. fileciteturn7file0turn7file5
- `seg_weights_from_mask` rounds uv to integer pixels and clips to mask bounds; if the point is out-of-bounds (e.g. x=528 on a 512-wide mask), the extracted patch is empty and it returns `(0,0,0)`. fileciteturn7file6

That exactly explains both patterns you saw in debug:
- `seg_w(bg=1,m0=0,m1=0)` when the wrong in-bounds location hits background,
- `seg_w(bg=0,m0=0,m1=0)` when uv is out-of-bounds.

With those weights, votes are `(0,0)` (since `obs_vote` requires `w0>thr` / `w1>thr`), and Stage B loses its segmentation signal. fileciteturn7file10turn7file0

### 3) A subtle but important point your results already hint at
Even with correct letterboxing, you can still get **(1,1) votes** due to the very low `vote_thr=0.01`. Example from your check:
- cam3_side mouse1 letterbox: `w_m0≈0.033` and `w_m1≈0.737` ⇒ both exceed 0.01 ⇒ vote becomes `(1,1)` (non-exclusive). fileciteturn7file1

That won’t break things by itself, but it reduces the number of “exclusive” decisions and pushes Stage B to lean more on continuity/centroid/axis—so once the coordinate mismatch is fixed, you’ll want to watch how often `(1,1)` appears in the StageB debug.

### What you can do *without changing the code yet* (pure inspection / data-side test)
If you want to confirm Stage B behaves sensibly once coordinates match **without editing the pipeline**, you can preprocess your 512×512 masks into 960×720 masks:

- crop out the padded region: `mask_crop = mask[64:448, :]`  (512×384)
- resize to 960×720 with **nearest-neighbor** (to preserve labels 0/1/2)

Then rerun Stage B pointing `--seg-dir` to the converted masks. If Stage B debug then shows meaningful `seg_w_m0/seg_w_m1` in B1, you’ve isolated the root cause cleanly.

If you want, I can provide a small conversion script that batch-processes `top/front/side` directories into a new `seg-dir` (still no changes to your main code).

Assistant: Nice — now the masks are in the **same pixel coordinate system** as the DLC UVs (720×960), and Stage B’s segmentation logic finally has something meaningful to work with.

### What changed in the logs (and why it matters)
1) **Mask shape now matches the camera frame**
- Stage B samples the segmentation mask at the *raw* DLC `uv` (no resizing/warping). That sampling is done by `seg_weights_from_mask(mask, obs.uv, kernel)`. fileciteturn9file2  
- `seg_weights_from_mask` assumes `uv` is already in the mask’s pixel space and extracts a Gaussian-weighted patch around it. fileciteturn8file7  
So with 720×960 masks, you’re finally sampling the correct pixels.

2) **`seg_w` now makes sense**
Example (frame 0, head):
- dlc_id=0: `(bg≈0.03, m0≈0.97, m1≈0.00)` → votes `(1,0)` and item becomes `exclusive=0`
- dlc_id=1: `(bg≈0.15, m0≈0.00, m1≈0.85)` → votes `(0,1)` and item becomes `exclusive=1`

That `exclusive` is computed exactly as: `exclusive_mouse_id(v0,v1)` where `v0/v1` are the summed binary votes across the obs. fileciteturn9file2

3) **Costs are no longer all zeros**
In your earlier broken run, the model had no anchors/centroids/axis and no exclusives → costs collapsed to 0 and the assignment degenerated into tie-breaking.

Now, because exclusives are present, `assignment_cost` adds strong bonus/penalty terms (`bonus_excl`, `penalty_excl`) and centroid/axis/continuity become active. fileciteturn9file2  
That’s exactly why you now see big spreads like `-0.31` vs `18.10` for the same 3D point assigned to different mice.

So: **Stage B is behaving qualitatively correctly now** (at least in terms of using the segmentation + 3D model as designed).

---

## What still looks “dangerous” for ID quality (even with correct resolution)
These are the likely remaining reasons it can still be worse than DLC in some sequences:

### 1) Very low `vote_thr=0.01` makes “(1,1)” votes common
`obs_vote(w0,w1,thr)` is literally `(w0>thr, w1>thr)`. fileciteturn9file2  
So if you have `m0=0.15, m1=0.07`, that becomes `(1,1)` even though it’s clearly leaning mouse0.

You see this a lot in your logs, especially side view points near boundaries (e.g. `m0=0.315, m1=0.004` stays clean, but `m0=0.365, m1=0.376` becomes `(1,1)` and destroys exclusivity).

**Impact:** items lose `exclusive` and then the decision leans more heavily on continuity/centroid/axis.

### 2) “Swap unknown” has a *conservative* anchor update rule
When `swap_state == UNKNOWN`, `build_mouse_models` **only uses anchor points if exclusives “separate cleanly”** (either one exclusive point, or two points with different exclusive labels). Otherwise it leaves anchors unset and later falls back to previous anchors. fileciteturn9file2

That can absolutely happen in your run:
- frame 1 head had two items with exclusives `[None, 1]` (because one head got a mixed vote and lost exclusivity).  
- Under the “swap unknown” conservative rule, that **does not update head for mouse0**, so mouse0 head can fall back to the previous frame’s head. fileciteturn9file2

**Impact:** the centroid/axis model can become stale for one mouse, which can mislead assignments for non-anchor bodyparts (since centroid/axis terms are always applied when available). fileciteturn9file2

### 3) Swap decision is borderline-ambiguous
Your frame 1 swap diagnostic shows `|C_no - C_sw| = 3.750 < tau_swap=4.0`, so it remains `UNKNOWN`. That threshold is exactly how ambiguity is decided. fileciteturn9file2

**Impact:** you remain in the conservative anchor-update regime longer than you might expect.

---

## What I’d inspect next (still no code changes)
1) **Count how often anchors lose exclusivity** (head, tail_root) due to `(1,1)` votes. That’s the quickest route to unstable models under `swap_state=UNKNOWN`.
2) Look at frames where IDs “go bad” and check whether just before the failure you see:
   - anchor exclusives become `None`, and/or
   - swap stays `UNKNOWN`, and/or
   - centroid/axis terms dominate because exclusives disappeared.

If you paste the debug excerpt from the first frame where you *visually* see the IDs become wrong, I can pinpoint whether it’s primarily:
- weak/mixed segmentation votes (threshold issue),
- conservative anchor updating while swap is UNKNOWN,
- or continuity overpowering segmentation in a specific situation.

(But at this point, the *original* showstopper — mismatched segmentation resolution — is fixed.)

Assistant: What your **frame 959** debug log is showing is *not* “Stage B flipped even though the mice are far apart” — it’s “Stage B had **no temporal context**, so it could not do the homogenization step, and it simply followed the per-frame segmentation labels.”

### 1) You ran frame 959 essentially “cold start” (no previous state)
In your command you only process `[959, 960)`, and `Mouse3DReconstructor` starts with a fresh `PrevState()` every run. fileciteturn11file3

That’s exactly consistent with the Stage B line:

- `SWAP decide usable=False (no previous anchors). prev_swap=UNKNOWN swap_age=999999`

`decide_swap_state()` declares continuity “usable” only if it has **previous anchors** (`prev_state.anchor_prev[...]` not None). If not, it returns `UNKNOWN` (unless it’s within a short carry window). fileciteturn11file0

So at frame 959 in *this run*, Stage B **cannot** use continuity to stabilize IDs across time.

### 2) With no continuity, Stage B mostly becomes “segmentation labeling = identity”
Two mechanics make this very direct:

**A) “Exclusive” segmentation is treated as strong evidence**
- Votes are `(w_m0>thr, w_m1>thr)` with `thr=0.01`. fileciteturn11file0  
- If summed votes are one-sided, `exclusive_mouse_id()` returns 0 or 1. fileciteturn11file0  
- In `assignment_cost()`, matching `exclusive` gives a bonus, mismatch gets a large penalty (`penalty_excl=9`, `bonus_excl=0.5`). fileciteturn11file0

**B) For single-3D items, exclusives are used *directly***
If a bodypart has only one triangulated 3D point in that frame, the code sets `mouse_id = exclusive` immediately (no continuity involved). fileciteturn11file0

**C) Continuity term is literally skipped if there is no previous**
`assignment_cost()` adds the continuity term only if `prev_state.prev[bodypart][mouse_id]` exists. fileciteturn11file0  
In your frame 959 run, those are empty → the continuity term contributes **0** for everything.

So if your segmentation model’s **instance IDs (labels 1 vs 2)** are swapped relative to what you consider “mouse0/mouse1” at that moment, Stage B will happily output the swapped IDs — because it has no past to contradict it.

### 3) Your own log already reveals the “swap” is driven by segmentation labels, not proximity
Look at the anchors in frame 959:

- `MODEL anchors_now part=head n=2 exclusives=[1, 0]`
- `MODEL anchors_now part=tail_root n=2 exclusives=[0, 1]`
- Then, because swap_state is UNKNOWN, `build_mouse_models()` uses **only exclusives** (conservative rule) to assign anchors to mouse 0/1. fileciteturn11file0

That means the *identity model itself* (centroid/axis) is built from segmentation-exclusive IDs in this frame — again, no temporal stabilization.

### 4) Why it *looks* like a sudden “ID change” even when mice are far apart
Two common reasons:

1) **Segmentation labels are not temporally consistent.**  
Many segmentation pipelines label instances arbitrarily each frame (or can swap 1↔2). If that happens at frame 959, Stage B will flip too — unless continuity is active to resist it (which it wasn’t in your isolated debug run).

2) **The numeric meaning of `mouse_id` is not “canonical” unless you run continuously.**  
Because `mouse_id` is produced from a combination of segmentation + continuity, starting at frame 959 with an empty `PrevState()` means mouse_id=0/1 are effectively re-initialized based on that frame’s segmentation evidence.

### The key takeaway
Your current debug run at frame 959 **cannot explain** a flip that happens in the *full* run, because it does not include the prior frames that provide continuity. It’s showing what Stage B does when continuity is unavailable: it follows segmentation exclusives. fileciteturn11file0turn11file3

If you want to diagnose a true “flip at 959” during a normal run, you need debug output for a short window that **includes earlier frames** so `prev_state` is populated and swap/continuity costs become nonzero (that’s where the interesting arbitration happens).

Assistant: I implemented the optional **B7 “graph/skeleton plausibility check”** exactly as you described:

- **New CLI flag:** `--graph_model PATH`
- If provided, Stage B runs an extra step **after B6** that:
  1) checks **leaf-node** edge-length plausibility for each mouse and **frees** leaf assignments that violate the ratio bounds  
  2) tries the freed leaf on the **other mouse** and re-checks  
  3) if it still fails (or neighbor is missing), the leaf’s **mouse_id is dropped** (`mouse_id=None`), so that 3D point will not be recorded nor carried into continuity
- **No effect** if `--graph_model` is not passed.

Defaults (in `StageBParams`):
- `graph_min_ratio = 2/3`
- `graph_max_ratio = 1.5`
- `graph_leaf_only = True` (as you suggested)

It prints **B7-specific debug lines** with the prefix `[DEBUG stageB] ... B7 ...` when `--debug-stageB` is enabled.

## What B7 is actually checking (with your provided model)
Your uploaded model defines these edges:

- head ↔ nose_tip / left_ear_tip / right_ear_tip / left_front_paw / right_front_paw / tail_root  
- tail_root ↔ left_hind_paw / right_hind_paw / tail_tip

So B7 checks **only leaf nodes** (nose/ears/paws/tail_tip), each against its single neighbor (head or tail_root).  
For each checked edge it computes:

- `L_model = ||node_a - node_b||` from the template file
- `D_3d = ||P_a - P_b||` from the current 3D estimates
- `ratio = L_model / D_3d`

and requires `ratio ∈ [0.667, 1.5]` (equivalently `D_3d ∈ [0.667*L_model, 1.5*L_model]`).

---

## Stage B summary (B1–B6) as it behaves right now
Here’s the algorithmic structure (matching your debug labels and the code flow):

### B1 — Segmentation evidence at each 2D observation
For each Stage-A triangulation hypothesis (“item”) and for each of its camera observations:
- evaluate segmentation mask around the 2D point using a Gaussian window (`seg_radius`, `seg_sigma`)
- get weights: `(bg, m0, m1)`
- convert to a **binary vote** per mouse using `vote_thr`
- sum votes across the item’s observations → `item.vote = (sum_vote0, sum_vote1)`
- set `item.exclusive` if votes are one-sided (e.g. `(3,0)` ⇒ exclusive mouse 0)

### B2 — Swap decision (global “NOSWAP/SWAP/UNKNOWN”)
Uses the **anchor parts** (default `head`, `tail_root`) and previous state to decide if the global identity assignment should be interpreted as swapped. If there’s not enough anchor continuity, it remains `UNKNOWN`.

### B3 — Build a simple per-mouse 3D model
Per mouse, constructs:
- `head`, `tail_root` anchors (from current exclusives if possible, else from previous anchors)
- `centroid` (midpoint head↔tail_root or fallback)
- `axis_line` (tail_root → head direction) if both available

### B4 — Assign each triangulated 3D item to mouse 0/1
For each bodypart:
- if there are **two** 3D items: choose the lower total cost between assignments (A→0,B→1) vs (A→1,B→0)
- if there is **one** 3D item: assign by exclusive if present, otherwise by cost comparison

Cost combines:
- exclusive bonus/penalty (can dominate)
- continuity vs predicted previous position
- distance to centroid
- distance to axis line

### B5 — Per-camera DLC-id mapping (dlc_id → canonical mouse)
After B4, for each camera it collects pairs `(dlc_id, resolved_mouse_id)` and chooses a majority mapping per dlc_id.

### B6 — Assign “single” (non-triangulated) observations
Singles have no 3D point; they get a mouse_id by:
1) exclusive segmentation (if present), else
2) **only if votes are exactly (0,0)**: use the per-camera mapping from B5, else
3) fall back to continuity (project previous 3D positions into the camera and pick closer)

### B7 — (new) Graph plausibility post-check
Only runs if `--graph_model` is provided.

---

## Why frame 959 can flip IDs even when mice are far apart
From your frame-959 debug excerpt, two mechanisms stand out that can produce “unreasonable” switches even when the animals are well separated in 3D:

### 1) You’re running frame 959 in isolation (no prior state) ⇒ swap logic can’t stabilize identities
In your command you process only `959..960`. That means at frame 959:
- `prev_state` is essentially empty
- B2 reports “no previous anchors” ⇒ `swap_state=UNKNOWN`
- so the entire “mouse0 vs mouse1” interpretation is driven mostly by **current-frame exclusives** (segmentation votes), not continuity

This can look like a “switch” even when it’s simply “no temporal reference; current frame decides.”

### 2) Votes can become “exclusive” even when the window is mostly background
You have multiple cases like:
- `seg_w(bg=0.933,m0=0.067,m1=0.000) vote=(1,0)` (because `0.067 > vote_thr=0.01`)

That means:
- a point that is **~93% background** still contributes a **full positive vote** for mouse0
- across 2–3 cameras, that quickly becomes `(2,0)` or `(3,0)` ⇒ `exclusive=0`
- then B4’s **exclusive penalty/bonus** heavily biases the assignment toward that mouse, even if geometry/centroid/axis would suggest otherwise

This exact pattern is a prime suspect for “worse than DLC”: a tiny segmentation bleed-through (or a single mislabeled pixel cluster within the Gaussian window) can hard-force an identity.

### 3) Disabled bbox gate increases chance of “valid reprojection, wrong 3D location”
You mentioned that rays can cross “far away” and still yield a plausible 3D point. In your current `run_mouse3d.py`, Stage A sets:
- `bbox_margin_mm = 1000.0` (effectively disables the bounding/cage plausibility gate)

So mismatched cross-mouse observation triplets can still yield a low-RMS BA solution at a physically implausible location, which then can pollute B4/B5/B6 decisions. B7 is meant to catch some of these via edge-length plausibility.

---

## Questions (to make B7 match your intent precisely)
1) **Units/scale:** Are the 3D reconstructions in **mm** (same scale as the template coordinates)?  
   If not, the ratio test is still scale-invariant-ish, but the tolerances may need adjustment.

2) **Neighbor missing case:** Right now:
   - if a leaf has no neighbor 3D point for its current mouse, B7 **skips** freeing it (can’t test)
   - but if a freed leaf is tried on the other mouse and the neighbor is missing there, it is **dropped**
   
   Is that what you want, or should “missing neighbor” also drop the leaf immediately to be conservative?

3) **Front paw edges to head:** Your graph connects `left/right_front_paw` directly to `head`. That length can vary a lot during locomotion.  
   Are you okay with potentially dropping many paw points, or should we later add per-edge tolerances (wider for paws)?

If you answer those, I can tune *only the B7 rule behavior* (still leaving B1–B6 unchanged), but for now the implementation follows your description with conservative defaults.

User: ref. 1: it is all mm
ref. 3: The current model does not move the paws much in the simulation. So the ratio interval should be fine.

ref 2: As there are disconnected body parts so far, it would be more plausible to keep the part with the other mouse, if it is consistent with the model. 

For that we could add another test. From the assigned model parts a rigid 3D transform of the model could be estimated. If successful the model's body part could be transformed to world coordinates and the resulting co-ordinates could be compared with the estimated coordinates. If close enough the single body part could be kept with that mouse id. I guess this is a reasonable argument for being logical as well as conservative and not get into dropping points that have not been dropped so far (singles existing before B7 already).

Assistant: Got it — with **mm units** and “paws not moving much” in your simulation, the rigid-template idea is a strong, conservative fallback for **disconnected leaf nodes**.

I implemented this as an extension to **B7** (still only active when `--graph_model` is provided):

- B7 still does the **leaf edge-length ratio test** using your graph edges (e.g., `head–nose_tip`, `tail_root–tail_tip`, etc.). fileciteturn12file0  
- **New fallback (within B7):** if the neighbor 3D point is missing, or if edge checks don’t resolve things, Stage B fits a **rigid model→world transform** per mouse (Kabsch) from the currently assigned 3D points and checks whether the leaf point is close to its **predicted transformed template position**.

This matches your goal:
- avoid “dropping” points just because the neighbor is absent,
- keep/switch a disconnected point to the mouse whose rigid-fitted model explains it better,
- still remove “random” triangulated points that land far away from any plausible rigid fit.

### Behavior details (conservative defaults)
When `--graph_model` is set, after B6:

1) **Leaf edge test (as before)**  
For each leaf node (degree 1), if both leaf and its neighbor have 3D points for that mouse:
- compute `ratio = L_model / D_3d`
- require `ratio ∈ [0.667, 1.5]` (your earlier suggestion)

2) **If an edge violation occurs**
- free the leaf’s assignment and try the other mouse using the same edge test
- if that still doesn’t resolve it, use the rigid fallback (below)

3) **If neighbor is missing (disconnected leaf)**
- try the other mouse’s neighbor edge test if available
- otherwise use the rigid fallback (below)

4) **Rigid fallback**
- For each mouse, estimate a rigid transform from the assigned 3D points that exist in the model (needs ≥3 non-collinear points; typical is fine in your sim).
- Predict where the leaf *should* be: `P_pred = R * model_leaf + t`
- Compare `err = ||P_leaf - P_pred||` to a tolerance:
  - `err <= min(graph_rigid_tol_mm_max, graph_rigid_tol_ratio * L_model_leaf_edge)`
  - defaults: `graph_rigid_tol_ratio=1.0`, `graph_rigid_tol_mm_max=35mm`

5) **If still undecidable**
- conservative default: **keep the current assignment** (so B7 doesn’t start dropping lots of points just because the rigid fit was unavailable that frame).
- you can flip that later via `graph_drop_if_undecidable=True` (currently a StageBParams field; no CLI yet).

## Comment on robustness
This rigid fallback is conservative, but there’s one important nuance:

- The rigid transform is fit from the **current assignments**. If a frame is already badly corrupted (many wrong assignments), the fit can “explain” the wrong configuration too.  
In your described regime (“majority of parts correct across cams”), this tends to work well: the fit anchors to the consistent majority, and outliers (random triangulations / wrong leaf IDs) get large residuals and are switched or suppressed.

If you want later, the next “safe” improvement would be to fit the rigid transform only from “high-confidence” points (e.g., anchor parts + exclusives), but I did **not** enforce that here to keep the behavior close to your current pipeline logic.

## What B7 does now (with your ref. 2 preference)
B7 runs **after B6** and only touches **3D points** (`P != None`). Singles are untouched.

For **leaf nodes** (degree-1 in the model graph):

1) **Edge ratio check (leaf ↔ its neighbor)**  
If neighbor exists for that mouse, it enforces  
`ratio = L_model / D_3d ∈ [0.667, 1.5]`  
If the leaf violates it, it gets **freed** and tried on the other mouse.

2) **If neighbor is missing (disconnected leaf)**  
Instead of dropping it immediately, B7 uses your proposed conservative fallback:

3) **Rigid-template fallback (Kabsch fit per mouse)**  
From all currently assigned points that exist in the graph model, estimate a rigid transform `world ≈ R·model + t`.  
Then predict where the leaf *should* be and compare to measured leaf position.  
If it matches (default tolerance: `min(35mm, 1.0 * model_edge_length)`), the leaf is **kept** (or reassigned to the other mouse if that fits better).

4) **If a leaf failed edge constraints and can’t be rescued by other-id edge check or rigid fit**  
It is dropped (`mouse_id=None`) → not recorded and not used for continuity.

So: **violating leaves get removed**, but **disconnected leaves are kept if they are rigidly consistent** (your conservative requirement).

## Two small questions (so B7 matches your intent precisely)
1) **Duplicates:** occasionally Stage A can output 2 triangulated hypotheses for the same bodypart. In B7 I keep the *first* `(mouse_id, bodypart)` item as the representative for edge/rigid checks (to avoid changing selection logic).  
Do you want me to instead prefer the one with lower `rms_px` for B7’s internal checks (doesn’t change B1–B6, but does change which of the two duplicates is considered “the” leaf)?

2) **Undecidable disconnected leaf:** if a leaf has no neighbor and rigid fit can’t be estimated (too few assigned points), I currently **keep it with its original mouse** by default (unless you later flip a parameter in code).  
Is that what you want, or should it be dropped in that scenario?

If you answer those, I can tune only the B7 logic (still leaving B1–B6 untouched).

User: ref1: Why would there be two output points from Stage A? I would prefer the triplet reconstruction. Or if it is all triplets or pairs the one with the smaller rms_px.

ref 2: Keep it for now.

Assistant: Makes sense.

### Why Stage A can output “two 3D points” for the same bodypart
That is **by design**: Stage A tries to reconstruct **up to two disjoint hypotheses** per bodypart (interpreted as “mouse0 candidate” + “mouse1 candidate”), using disjointness in `(cam_idx, dlc_id)` so it doesn’t reuse the same DLC individual in the same camera for both hypotheses. Concretely:

- It generates all triplet and pair hypotheses over dlc_id∈{0,1} across cams
- Evaluates them and then calls `select_best_disjoint(..., max_points=2)` which explicitly searches over selecting **up to 2** disjoint hypotheses fileciteturn18file1
- Any unused observations become “single” items (P=None) fileciteturn18file1

So at a frame like your 959 example, seeing one bodypart with e.g. one **triplet** (mouse A) and one **pair** (mouse B) is normal: one mouse might be missing a camera observation or fails gates, so the best disjoint set becomes “triplet + pair”.

## Your requested B7 selection rule (triplet preferred, else lower RMS)
Implemented.

B7 internally needs a single representative 3D point per `(mouse_id, bodypart)` for:
- the edge checks (leaf↔neighbor)
- the rigid template fit

I updated `_collect_assigned_3d()` so that if there are multiple candidates for the same `(mouse_id, bodypart)` (rare, but possible via later reassignment steps), it chooses:

1) **triplet** over **pair** over anything else  
2) if same kind: **smaller `rms_px`** wins (when available)

This exactly matches your preference.

### Ref 2: disconnected leaf handling
Also aligned: if a leaf is disconnected (neighbor missing) and rigid fit can’t decide, it is **kept** with its current assignment (conservative default), as you requested.

Assistant: Yes — your interpretation is basically correct, and the sequence is logical given how B7 is written.

### What happened to `right_ear_tip` in frame 962 (mouse 0)
1) **Edge check fails (barely)**
- `L=13.216`, `D=8.778` ⇒ `ratio = 13.216 / 8.778 = 1.506`
- your max is `1.5`, so it fails by **0.006**.  
That triggers:
- `B7 free leaf=right_ear_tip from m=0 reason=violation`

So at this point, **the assignment is temporarily removed** from mouse 0.

2) **“Try other mouse” step cannot proceed**
- `B7 try_other ... -> other has leaf already; keep free`

Meaning: mouse 1 already has its own `right_ear_tip` 3D item assigned, so B7 doesn’t “steal” it or compare two options. It leaves mouse0’s right_ear_tip **unassigned** (“free”) and moves on.

3) **Rigid fallback step decides it still belongs to mouse 0**
- `B7 rigid_try leaf=right_ear_tip err0=3.099 err1=219.179 tol=13.216`
- `B7 rigid_assign leaf=right_ear_tip -> m=0 err=3.099`

This means: using the rigid model fit for each mouse, the **predicted** right_ear_tip position for mouse 0 is only 3.1 mm away from the observed 3D point, while for mouse 1 it’s ~219 mm away. Since 3.1 mm is well below the tolerance (13.216 mm here), B7 re-attaches the leaf to mouse 0.

So yes: **it was freed due to the edge-ratio test, and then re-assigned back to the same mouse by the rigid-template consistency test.**

That’s expected, and it can happen in two common situations:
- the edge-length violation is marginal (like yours), and/or
- the neighbor used for the edge test (here: `head`) is slightly off, so the single edge distance is misleading, but the **global rigid fit** across many bodyparts supports the point strongly.

### Why the tolerance is `13.216` here
You’re seeing `tol=13.216`, which equals `L` for that edge. That matches the default I used:
- `tol = min(35mm, graph_rigid_tol_ratio * L)` with `graph_rigid_tol_ratio=1.0`

So rigid acceptance here means “within about one template edge length” (and capped at 35 mm). With `err0=3.1mm`, it’s a very confident keep.

### What about `left_hind_paw` and `right_hind_paw` being freed and then assigned back to mouse 1?
Same pattern, different reason:
- They were freed due to `reason=no_neighbor` (meaning: for mouse 1, the neighbor `tail_root` wasn’t available as an assigned 3D point at that moment, so the edge test couldn’t be evaluated).
- “Try other” also couldn’t proceed because the other mouse already had those leaves.
- Rigid fit then clearly supports mouse 1 (`err1≈3mm`, `err0≈180–200mm`), so they get assigned back to mouse 1.

### Is this “too lenient”?
In this specific case, it’s probably exactly what you want:
- The edge test fails by a hair (1.506 vs 1.5).
- The rigid fit says “this point is geometrically consistent with mouse 0’s whole-body configuration.”

If you later decide you want the edge test to be “hard” (i.e., once freed for violation, only allow switching to the other mouse, not returning), we can do that — but it would make the method less conservative and more likely to drop points unnecessarily near thresholds.

If you paste the surrounding B7 lines where the head and right_ear_tip 3D coordinates are shown (or tell me which bodyparts were used in the rigid fit), I can also explain *why* that specific head↔ear distance came out short (8.8 mm) while the rigid model still fits.

Assistant: You’re reading the logs correctly.

### Why `tol=13.216` happened
- Yes: **`L` is the template edge length** (in mm) for that leaf↔neighbor edge (e.g. `head–right_ear_tip`).  
- The rigid fallback tolerance was computed as:

\[
\text{tol}=\min(\text{graph\_rigid\_tol\_mm\_max},\ \text{graph\_rigid\_tol\_ratio}\cdot L)
\]

With the old defaults (`graph_rigid_tol_ratio=1`, `graph_rigid_tol_mm_max=35`), and for ears where `L≈13.216`, you get `tol=min(35, 13.216)=13.216`.

So you’re right to question it: **for short edges (<35 mm), this effectively allowed up to ~one full edge-length of absolute positional error**, which is much looser than “a couple mm”.

Also: this tolerance is **not** “allow the edge to be twice as long.” It’s an **absolute positional error** between the observed leaf point and the rigidly predicted leaf point. But using `L` as the tolerance scale can indeed be far too permissive in a clean simulation.

### Why `right_ear_tip` got reassigned to the *same* mouse
In both of your examples:

1) Edge check fails → leaf is freed (temporarily).
2) “Try other” can’t move it because the other mouse already has that leaf → it stays unassigned.
3) Rigid fallback then says: this freed leaf is still much closer to mouse0’s rigidly predicted ear than mouse1’s (mouse1 is ~220 mm away), and it was within the (too-large) tolerance → it gets assigned back to mouse0.

That’s exactly what you’re seeing with:

- frame 966: `err0=7.163`, `tol=13.216` ⇒ accepted ⇒ assigned back to mouse0

And you’re saying: **no, 7 mm shouldn’t pass if the edge check failed hard** (ratio 1.864).

I agree.

---

## Change applied: make rigid tolerance tight (default 5 mm)
I changed the default in `StageBParams`:

- `graph_rigid_tol_mm_max: float = 5.0` (was 35.0)

This means for essentially all your leaf edges (L > 5 mm), the rigid fallback now uses **tol=5.0 mm**.

So your frame 966 case would now behave like you want:
- edge violation frees `right_ear_tip`
- “try_other” still can’t move it (duplicate on other mouse)
- rigid error `7.163 > 5.0` ⇒ **no rigid reassign**
- because it was freed for `reason=violation`, it will be **dropped** (`mouse_id=None`) rather than silently kept.

### Added CLI override (so you can tune without editing code)
In `run_mouse3d.py` I added:

- `--graph-rigid-tol-mm-max <float>`
- `--graph-rigid-tol-ratio <float>`

So you can explicitly do:
```bash
python run_mouse3d.py ... \
  --graph_model ../../mouse_sim/mouse3_mesh_shortTail.txt \
  --graph-rigid-tol-mm-max 5
```

### Optional stricter policy (if you want it later)
If you decide “rigid fallback should **never** rescue a leaf freed for `violation` (only for `no_neighbor`)”, that’s a one-line logic change in B7. I didn’t do that yet because you didn’t explicitly request it, but it would align with your “don’t override edge failures” philosophy even more strongly than tightening tol.

User: There are some cases where the head is graph model connected to the other mouse's tail_root. So the two halve animals are connected the wrong way mouse0 front goes with mouse1 back and vice versa. That should be avoidable with the segment check on the head and tail. Surprisingly it nevertheless happens. May this be due to the continuity constraint being carried over from frame to frame? Color being equivalent with segment index should dominate here. (I would not like to try the model check here, as it could result in an identity switch when segment index is not considered?)

Assistant: Yes — that “two half animals stitched together” failure mode is entirely explainable from the current Stage-B logic, and **it can happen even when `swap_state=NOSWAP` most of the time**.

### Why it can happen at all
Stage B builds the per-mouse 3D model (centroid + axis) from **anchors** (`head`, `tail_root`) *before* it assigns all bodyparts. Crucially:

1) **Head and tail_root anchors are assigned independently**
`build_mouse_models()` loops over `params.anchor_parts` and for each part it decides which current 3D point is mouse0 vs mouse1 based on motion targets + a small exclusive bias. There is no constraint that the head decision and the tail_root decision must be consistent with each other. fileciteturn19file3

That means it’s possible to end up with:
- mouse0 gets **head from mouse A**
- mouse0 gets **tail_root from mouse B**
(and the opposite for mouse1)

Once that happens, the “mouse0 axis line” spans across animals, so a bunch of other bodyparts can be pulled into the wrong ID even if the animals are far apart.

2) **Segmentation exclusives do not dominate in the anchor/swap logic**
Even if the segmentation vote for head/tail_root is “exclusive”, anchor assignment (and swap decision) only uses a *weak* bias:
- `penalty_excl_swap = 1.0`, `bonus_excl_swap = 0.25` fileciteturn19file1

But the motion term is quadratic: `(||P - T|| / sigma_motion_anchor)^2` with `sigma_motion_anchor=8mm`. fileciteturn19file0turn19file1

So a modest motion mismatch (say 20 mm) contributes about `(20/8)^2 ≈ 6.25`, which easily overwhelms a `+1` penalty. In other words: **continuity can override segmentation** for anchors.

3) **`NOSWAP` doesn’t prevent this**
`swap_state` is global (NOSWAP/SWAP/UNKNOWN), but the head and tail_root per-part assignments still consider both pairings (`cA` vs `cB`) *within that swap hypothesis*. So you can keep `NOSWAP` globally and still “locally swap” the tail_root assignment if continuity prefers it.

### So is it “due to continuity being carried frame-to-frame”?
Very plausibly, yes.

If in some earlier frame the anchor assignment got slightly wrong for one anchor (e.g., tail_root), then the predicted targets `T0/T1` for later frames will keep pulling that anchor the wrong way, because the swap/anchor logic treats exclusives weakly. fileciteturn19file0turn19file3

### Is this caused by B7 rigid/graph checking?
Not the specific failure you describe:
- Your B7 is **leaf-only**, and head/tail_root are **not leaves** in the template graph. fileciteturn20file1  
So B7 does not touch head/tail_root and cannot directly “switch identity” at the anchor level.

### How to verify this diagnosis quickly in logs
Pick a frame where you see the “front/back cross” visually, and compare:

1) B1 exclusives for `head` and `tail_root` (should usually be clean).
2) The `MODEL anchor_assigned part=head ...` and `MODEL anchor_assigned part=tail_root ...` lines.

If you see the telltale pattern:
- head exclusives suggest one mapping
- tail_root exclusives suggest the same mapping
but `anchor_assigned` ends up inconsistent between head and tail_root, then the root cause is exactly the **independent anchor assignment under continuity**.

### Why your intuition (“segment index should dominate”) doesn’t hold right now
Segmentation *does* dominate later in B4/B6 for most bodyparts via `penalty_excl=9.0` and `bonus_excl=0.5` (much stronger). fileciteturn19file1  
But for *anchors/swap*, the code intentionally uses much weaker weights (`penalty_excl_swap=1.0`). fileciteturn19file1  
So the very place where “front/back stitching” is created is also the place where segmentation is least enforced.

If you want, paste just the `head` + `tail_root` sections (B1 + MODEL anchor_assigned) from one bad frame, and I’ll point to the exact inequality that let head and tail_root choose opposite pairings.

User: At frame 195 everything is fine. At frame  199 the front and back halves are assigned wrongly. Somewhere in between it happens.

Assistant: What you’re seeing (“mouse0 front + mouse1 back” chimera) is very plausibly a **tail_root anchor going wrong one frame earlier**, and then the **continuity prediction amplifying that error**.

### 1) It doesn’t start at frame 199 — it starts at frame 198 (in your 195→199 run)

From the debug you uploaded (`cleaningdebug.out`), the anchor assignments evolve like this:

- **Frame 197 tail_root**  
  `m0=(241.288, 118.427, 18.598)`  
  `m1=(180.554, 164.401, 17.517)`

- **Frame 198 tail_root**  
  `m0=(183.926, 116.530, 29.971)`  ← *this is the “jump”*  
  `m1=(182.287, 166.379, 16.633)`

So **mouse0’s tail_root “teleports” ~57 mm in X** between frames 197 and 198. That’s exactly the kind of jump you described from occasional “accidental” 3D intersections.

Once that happens, your continuity predictor is literally:

- `predict(prev1, prev2) = prev1 + (prev1 - prev2)` fileciteturn24file0

So at frame 199 the predicted target for mouse0 tail_root becomes:

- `T0 = 183.926 + (183.926 − 241.288) = 126.563` (in X)

…and your Stage B debug at frame 199 indeed shows `T0=(126.563,114.634,41.343)` for tail_root in the SWAP-cost print. That confirms the mechanism: **a single bad anchor at frame 198 creates a huge velocity vector, which then skews the next frame’s decisions**.

### 2) Why this can produce “front/back halves swapped” even when `swap_state=NOSWAP`

This is the key conceptual point:

- `swap_state` (NOSWAP/SWAP/UNKNOWN) is only a **global identity bookkeeping** about whether mouse0/mouse1 swapped relative to previous frames.
- **It does not enforce that head and tail_root chosen for mouse0 come from the same physical animal.**

In `build_mouse_models()`, **head and tail_root are assigned independently** (each anchor part is matched to predicted targets separately) fileciteturn24file0. So you can get:

- head(m0) = correct mouse0 head  
- tail_root(m0) = actually mouse1 tail_root (or a spurious 3D tail_root near mouse1)  

…without any “global swap” ever triggering.

That’s exactly a “chimera” failure mode: **NOSWAP can be true while the per-frame anchor pairing is internally inconsistent**.

### 3) “But segmentation color should dominate head & tail!” — why it sometimes doesn’t

Segmentation only becomes “hard” in your pipeline when the per-item vote becomes *exclusive*:

- `exclusive_mouse_id(v0,v1)` returns 0 only if `v0>0 && v1==0`, returns 1 only if `v1>0 && v0==0`, else `None`. fileciteturn24file0

So if tail_root (or head) has mixed evidence (both votes >0, or both 0), **exclusive becomes None** and segmentation stops being a decisive constraint for that item.

On top of that, even when exclusives exist, **swap/anchor matching uses only a weak bias**:

- `penalty_excl_swap=1.0`, `bonus_excl_swap=0.25` (used in swap decision and anchor assignment) fileciteturn24file0  
while the later per-bodypart assignment (B4) uses stronger values:
- `penalty_excl=9.0`, `bonus_excl=0.5` fileciteturn24file0

So in exactly the situation you have at frame ~198 (one anchor candidate spurious; the other somewhat wrong; segmentation not cleanly exclusive), the continuity term can win and the anchor can flip.

### 4) Why this can look like “head connected to the other mouse’s tail_root”

Because the model is built from **(head, tail_root)** per mouse, if the tail_root anchor goes wrong for mouse0 while the head anchor stays correct, your axis/centroid will be computed from mismatched endpoints. That then contaminates B4 costs for other bodyparts too (centroid/axis proximity), making the “half-and-half” assignment persist.

### 5) Why your current B7 won’t prevent this (even if it’s enabled)

Your B7 is currently **leaf-only** (ears/paws/nose/tail_tip), and **does not check the head–tail_root edge**, even though your graph contains it:

`head tail_root` is explicitly in the model edges. fileciteturn22file5

So the exact failure you’re describing (head ↔ tail_root cross-pairing) is *outside* what B7 is currently testing. That’s consistent with “surprisingly it nevertheless happens”.

## What I would inspect next (no code changes yet)

1) **Frame 198, tail_root Stage A candidates**  
   You want to know whether the “bad” tail_root is:
   - a real tail_root but assigned to wrong identity because segmentation wasn’t exclusive, **or**
   - a genuinely spurious 3D point that happens to have low reprojection RMS.

   Concretely: for frame 198 tail_root, print both candidates’ `rms_px`, `max_err_px`, and their per-camera obs (uv + seg_w). If one candidate has “plausible RMS but nonsense 3D location”, you’ve found your “rays pass far away” artifact.

2) **Check whether tail_root exclusives become `None` at the transition**
   Your own frame 198 key line already hints this kind of situation (tail_root exclusives were not cleanly separated in that region of your run). When exclusives aren’t clean, the system is effectively running on continuity.

3) **Interpret NOSWAP correctly**
   Seeing mostly NOSWAP in the summary is compatible with frequent internal anchor mismatches. NOSWAP only says “don’t globally relabel the mice”, not “anchors are coherent”.

Assistant: Yes — this is exactly the kind of evidence I meant. And in your paste there’s a very specific “smoking gun” for the *front/back halves stitched across mice* failure mode.

## What your frame=198 B1 log is saying

### 1) Head is clean and consistent (good anchor)
Your `head` triplet has `vote_sum=(3,0)` → `exclusive=0`. That means **all three camera observations vote mouse0**, so segmentation is unambiguous for that head hypothesis.

### 2) Tail_root has **two competing 3D hypotheses**, and one is *cross-mouse*
You have **two `tail_root` pair items**:

**Tail_root hypothesis A (exclusive mouse1):**
```
cam1_top dlc_id=1  seg -> mouse1   vote (0,1)
cam3_side dlc_id=0 seg -> mouse1   vote (0,1)
vote_sum=(0,2) exclusive=1
```
That’s internally consistent: both observations vote mouse1.

**Tail_root hypothesis B (ambiguous / mixed):**
```
cam2_front dlc_id=0 seg -> mouse1   vote (0,1)   (seg_w ... m1=1.000)
cam3_side  dlc_id=1 seg -> mouse0   vote (1,0)   (seg_w ... m0=0.709)
vote_sum=(1,1) exclusive=None
```
This is *not* a “weak segmentation” case — it’s a **hard contradiction across cameras**: one obs votes mouse1, the other votes mouse0, so the item cannot become exclusive by construction. The code only sets `exclusive` if `(v0>0,v1==0)` or `(v1>0,v0==0)`. fileciteturn27file0

Interpretation: **Stage A constructed a tail_root 3D point using one observation that lies on mouse1 in cam2_front and another that lies on mouse0 in cam3_side.** That is literally a “hybrid” hypothesis, and its 3D intersection can land somewhere arbitrary (including between animals), even with decent reprojection.

This is exactly the situation that can “snap” one anchor (tail_root) onto the wrong animal while the other anchor (head) stays correct.

## Why segmentation “should dominate” but doesn’t (yet) in this specific step

Two reasons in the current logic:

1) **Segmentation only dominates if it becomes `exclusive`.** Your mixed tail_root hypothesis never becomes exclusive, by definition. fileciteturn27file0

2) **Anchor assignment and swap decision only apply a *weak* exclusive bias**, while continuity distance is quadratic. In `StageBParams`, the swap/anchor exclusivity weights are `penalty_excl_swap=1.0` and `bonus_excl_swap=0.25`. fileciteturn27file0  
So if continuity “likes” the mixed hypothesis (because of a bad predicted target or a spurious previous point), it can win despite segmentation.

Also important: **head and tail_root are assigned independently** in `build_mouse_models()` and can fall back to previous anchors separately. fileciteturn27file0  
That’s the direct mechanism for producing “mouse0 head + mouse1 tail_root”.

## Why this can appear suddenly between 195 → 199
Once a wrong (or spurious) tail_root is accepted for one frame, the predictor is:
`Ppred = prev1 + (prev1 - prev2)` fileciteturn27file0  
So one bad point creates a “velocity” that can keep pulling the next frame’s tail_root assignment toward the wrong hypothesis.

## What to look at next (no code changes)
To pinpoint the exact frame where the chimera is created, the most informative next output is:

- `--debug_stageA` for **tail_root** around frames 196–199, so you can see which 3D hypotheses Stage A actually *chose* (it prints CHOSEN hypotheses with `cams`, `dlc_ids`, `P`, `rms_px`, etc.). fileciteturn26file9  
The key question: **is the mixed tail_root hypothesis (front mouse1 + side mouse0) being selected as one of the two disjoint “chosen” 3D points, and does it sometimes have a better `rms_px` than the correct one?**

If you paste the Stage A `CHOSEN#...` lines for `tail_root` for frames 197, 198, 199, I can tell you which hypothesis is getting through, and whether the anchor assignment step is likely pairing it to the wrong mouse via continuity.

Assistant: Yes — applying the **graph check to the anchor edge `head ↔ tail_root`** is exactly the missing piece for the “two halves stitched together” failure mode.

Right now B7 is **leaf-only**, so it can’t catch the specific pathology you described, because the “chimera” is created when **`head(m0)` is paired with `tail_root(m1)`** (and vice versa). Your template explicitly contains the `head tail_root` edge, so it’s available for such a check. fileciteturn29file1

## Why it happens despite “segmentation should dominate”
Two mechanisms interact:

### 1) Stage A can produce a tail_root 3D hypothesis from mixed-mouse observations
In your frame 198 excerpt, you have a tail_root hypothesis with `vote_sum=(1,1)` and `exclusive=None` because one camera votes mouse1 and the other votes mouse0:

- cam2_front: `(0,1)`  
- cam3_side: `(1,0)`  
→ mixed votes, no exclusivity.

That *is* a “front/back stitching seed”: it is literally a cross-mouse 3D candidate.

### 2) Anchor matching uses continuity strongly, and segmentation only weakly
In the anchor/swap logic the exclusive bias is intentionally small (`penalty_excl_swap=1.0`, `bonus_excl_swap=0.25`) fileciteturn28file6, while the continuity term is quadratic in distance and uses a small sigma (`sigma_motion_anchor=8mm`). fileciteturn28file6  
So if continuity predicts the wrong tail_root (especially after one spurious 3D point), it can overpower segmentation *unless* the anchor candidate is truly exclusive and clean.

Also: head and tail_root are assigned **independently** when building the per-mouse model, so you can end up with head correct and tail_root wrong without any “global SWAP” being triggered. fileciteturn28file1turn28file5

So yes: continuity carried from frame to frame is a very plausible amplifier.

## Why adding B7 for `head↔tail_root` would help (and how to keep “color dominates”)
You can add an **anchor-consistency check** that:

1) uses only the **template head↔tail_root edge** length (from the graph model) fileciteturn29file1  
2) uses the **segmentation-derived exclusives** already computed in B1  
3) does **not** use rigid fitting for anchors (so it can’t introduce identity switches due to model-only reasoning)

A conservative, “color-first” rule that addresses your exact failure:

### Proposed B7-anchor rule (conceptual)
After B6 (and before any rigid fallback):

- For each mouse `m`, if both `head_m` and `tail_root_m` exist:
  - compute ratio `L_model / ||head_m - tail_root_m||`
  - require it within `[min_ratio, max_ratio]` (same bounds as leaf checks)

- Additionally, treat the segmentation label as dominant:
  - if `tail_root_m.exclusive` exists and `tail_root_m.exclusive != m`, mark it invalid immediately  
  - if the tail_root candidate has **mixed votes** (effectively `exclusive=None` but both sides voted at least once), treat it as *low-trust* for anchors

- If the head↔tail_root check fails for a mouse:
  - **do not move the head**
  - either (A) swap the two tail_root assignments **only if** each tail_root’s `exclusive` matches the destination mouse, **and** the swapped head↔tail_root edges pass  
  - else (B) drop (unassign) the offending tail_root for that frame and let continuity re-acquire next frame

This prevents the “mouse0 front + mouse1 back” axis from being constructed, while still respecting segmentation “color” as primary evidence.

## Even simpler (often enough): don’t let mixed-vote anchor candidates participate
You can also stop the chimera one step earlier with a segmentation-only filter:

- During anchor assignment (head/tail_root only), **ignore candidates with `exclusive=None` and vote_sum indicating both mice got votes** (i.e., the “(1,1) across cameras” situation).

That would have rejected your mixed tail_root hypothesis immediately, which is the direct precursor to the stitched animal.

## Why I agree you shouldn’t use the rigid model for anchors here
Your concern is valid: a rigid fit can “explain” the wrong identity if it’s allowed to dominate. For leaf nodes it’s a cleanup heuristic; for anchors it becomes an identity driver. So for head/tail_root I’d keep it segmentation + edge-length only.

Assistant: ### What’s going wrong between frames 195 → 199 (your “front/back halves swap”)

Using your `mouse3_mesh_shortTail.txt`, the model explicitly contains the **head–tail_root edge** (and therefore an expected length for that “spine” segment). fileciteturn32file0L1-L20

From that file, the template head–tail_root distance is about **52.5 mm** (in your mm world units). A reasonable plausibility band with your ratio limits (0.667…1.5) corresponds to roughly **35–79 mm**.

From your `cleaningdebug.out`, the **per-mouse head–tail_root distances** around the failure look like this:

- frame 195: m0 ≈ 52.5 mm (OK), m1 ≈ 53.1 mm (OK)  
- frame 196: m0 ≈ 52.0 mm (OK), m1 ≈ 52.3 mm (OK)  
- frame 197: m0 ≈ 54.5 mm (OK), m1 ≈ 49.7 mm (OK)  
- **frame 198: m0 ≈ 80.8 mm (FAIL, barely), m1 ≈ 50.1 mm (OK)**  
- **frame 199: m0 ≈ 102.6 mm (FAIL), m1 ≈ 137.3 mm (FAIL)**  

So the “chimera” starts **already at frame 198** (mouse0’s anchor pair becomes implausible), then fully collapses at 199.

Now the crucial smoking gun is also in your frame 198 Stage B log:

- frame 198 has **two tail_root 3D candidates** in B1:  
  - one with `exclusive=1` (clean segmentation support)  
  - one with `exclusive=None` (mixed/ambiguous seg votes)  
- but in B3 you get:  
  `MODEL anchor_assigned part=tail_root m0=(183.926,116.530,29.971) m1=(182.287,166.379,16.633)`  

That `m0` tail_root is exactly the “mixed” candidate (the one that *should be treated as suspicious*), and it’s what stretches head↔tail_root to ~80.8 mm.

### Why segmentation “should dominate”, but currently doesn’t (in anchors)

Stage B uses segmentation in **two different places**:

1) **Final per-bodypart assignment cost (B4)**  
   Segmentation “exclusive” is a *strong* term here (`penalty_excl=9`, optional `hard_exclusive`). fileciteturn32file1L23-L44

2) **Anchor continuity + swap decision (B2/B3)**  
   Here segmentation is only a **weak bias** (`penalty_excl_swap=1.0`, `bonus_excl_swap=0.25`). fileciteturn32file1L23-L44

So even if head/tail_root are “seg-exclusive”, B3 can still pick the wrong anchor pairing when continuity is strong, because the anchor assignment uses a *different*, much weaker exclusive penalty than B4. fileciteturn32file1L90-L206

That’s exactly the scenario you described: a spurious but “geometrically plausible” 3D tail_root (coming from rays that happen to pass near each other) can get pulled into the wrong mouse by continuity in B3, and then the **axis/centroid built from those anchors** will drag other parts into the same wrong front/back split.

### Why your current B7 doesn’t prevent the head↔tail_root “cross”

Your current B7 is focused on **leaf edges** (ears, paws, nose_tip, tail_tip). The head and tail_root are **not leaves**, and the algorithm has **no explicit constraint that head and tail_root must form a plausible spine segment**, even though the graph contains that edge. fileciteturn32file0L1-L20

So B7 can “clean” leaf nodes while leaving the **anchor pairing itself** in a physically impossible configuration.

---

## Why it makes sense to apply B7-type logic to head and tail_root

It’s a good idea — but the key is **where** it’s applied:

- If you apply it only **after B6**, you can fix the anchors for *the next frame’s continuity*, but you **won’t undo the wrong axis/centroid that already influenced B4** in the current frame.
- To prevent the front/back chimera, the check must run **before using the anchors to build the axis/centroid used in B4**, i.e. conceptually as a **B3.5 anchor gate**, or you must **rebuild the mouse models and rerun B4** when the anchor gate fires.

### What the “anchor gate” should do (conservative + segmentation-dominant)

Given your stated preference (“color/segment index should dominate; don’t let the model cause identity switches”), the safe rule-set is:

1) **Hard respect segmentation exclusives for anchors**  
   If head or tail_root has `exclusive=0/1`, do **not** allow it to be assigned to the other mouse in the anchor assignment step (B3).  
   Concretely: treat anchor exclusives as “hard_exclusive” during *anchor assignment*, not just in B4.

2) **Use the head–tail_root edge-length test as a veto**  
   After assigning anchors to each mouse, compute D(head, tail_root) and check the ratio bounds. If it fails:
   - prefer to **drop** the weaker/suspicious anchor (usually the one with `exclusive=None`, fewer cameras, worse rms),
   - fall back to previous anchor for that mouse (continuity), or mark axis invalid for this frame,
   - and (critically) **rebuild centroid/axis** before assigning other bodyparts.

This directly blocks your failure mode at frame 198:
- the tail_root candidate with `exclusive=None` would be rejected as anchor for mouse0 because it makes head–tail_root implausible,
- preventing the wrong axis that later glues mouse0’s head to mouse1’s back.

---

## About your “continuity carry-over” hypothesis

Yes: the behavior you’re seeing is absolutely consistent with continuity dominating the anchor choice, because anchor assignment uses `penalty_excl_swap=1.0` (weak) and continuity distances are squared and can easily dominate. fileciteturn32file1L90-L206

That’s also why you can see “NOSWAP” almost everywhere while still getting a front/back chimera: “NOSWAP” just says the global identity didn’t flip; it doesn’t prevent assigning a *bad* anchor to the wrong mouse if continuity prefers it.

   Is “mask value 1 == mouse0” and “mask value 2 == mouse1” true **across all 3 cameras and all frames**, i.e. never swapping?  
   If there’s any per-view or temporal label swapping, then making anchor exclusives “hard” will sometimes force the wrong identity.

2) **Do you want the anchor gate to *drop* implausible anchors (conservative) or to *swap anchors between mice* when that repairs the spine length?**  
   You sounded like you prefer conservative dropping over identity switches — I agree, but I want to confirm that swapping anchors is off the table unless segmentation also agrees.

3) For the spine length bound: do you want to keep your current ratio interval (0.667…1.5), or tighten it just for head–tail_root (since that edge is relatively stable)?

Assistant: Yes, extending **B7 to the `head–tail_root` edge** is the right lever for the “front of mouse0 glued to back of mouse1” failure mode — and your proposed “try_other both directions + segmentation vote dominance” is directionally sound.

Before coding, here are the key points, the open questions I need answered, and a couple of suggestions to make the rule both conservative and implementable without exploding complexity.

---

## What your proposal is really doing (in my words)
You want an **anchor-consistency repair** step that runs when the **head–tail_root** edge for a mouse fails the rigid length test.

Instead of just dropping one anchor, you want to consider alternative connections:

- If `head(m)` is currently connected to `tail_root(m)` and fails,
  - try `head(m)` with `tail_root(other)` (steal the other tail_root)
  - try `tail_root(m)` with `head(other)` (steal the other head)
  - also consider that the “other” connections might already exist but be bad, so they should be allowed to be replaced if they fail too.

Then use segmentation vote evidence (B1 votes / exclusives) to decide which pairing is most plausible. The stronger segmentation evidence should dominate the weaker node’s mouse id, and then propagate that id through its attached leaf nodes to preserve homogeneity.

That aims to **prevent anchor-level chimeras**, which is exactly where your current pipeline is vulnerable.

---

## Crucial observation from current code
Right now, the chimera happens because **B3 assigns head and tail_root independently** (no coupling constraint), and B7 (leaf-only) happens too late to prevent the wrong centroid/axis from influencing B4. fileciteturn33file0

So the anchor fix must occur **before** B4’s identity costs are computed, or else you must **rebuild the mouse model + rerun B4** after repairing anchors.

This is an important design choice.

---

## Open questions I need you to answer before I code

### Q1 — When exactly should this anchor repair run?
Pick one:

**A)** “B3.5”: run right after `build_mouse_models()` and before `B4`.  
This prevents the wrong centroid/axis from ever affecting B4. (My recommendation.)

**B)** “B7 after B6” (your wording suggests this), but then you must accept that B4 was computed using possibly wrong anchors; you’d only be stabilizing future frames (continuity), not fixing the current frame’s bodypart IDs.

If you want to fix the chimera **within the same frame**, you want **A**.

### Q2 — What is the acceptance criterion for a proposed new head↔tail_root pairing?
Right now you suggested two checks:
1) edge length in ratio bounds (you said it’s rigid so bounds can be tight)
2) segmentation “color” support using the B1 vote info

But “segmentation support” needs to be made precise. Options:

- **Strict exclusive match:** accept pair only if both nodes are exclusive and match the mouse id they’d be assigned to (strongest).
- **Vote dominance:** accept if the sum-vote favors that mouse id even if not exclusive (weaker).
- **Per-observation weights:** use summed `(w_m0, w_m1)` rather than binary votes (more nuanced, but a change in reasoning signal).

Which do you want? My suggestion: **strict exclusive match for anchors** (head and tail_root), because these are the foundation of the model and you explicitly want segmentation to dominate.

### Q3 — How tight is “rigid” for head↔tail_root?
Your current ratio interval is `[0.667, 1.5]`. For head↔tail_root you implied it’s rigid and you’re not worried.

Do you want a tighter band for this one edge, e.g. `[0.85, 1.15]` (≈ ±15%), while keeping the looser band for leaf edges?  
Or keep the global band?

### Q4 — If segmentation votes contradict the only geometrically plausible pairing, what wins?
Example: head is exclusive=0, tail_root is exclusive=1, but the only edge-length-valid pairing is head0↔tail_root1 (which violates segmentation).

Do you want to:
- refuse to change (drop one anchor / fall back to previous), or
- allow geometry to override segmentation?

Based on what you wrote: segmentation should dominate. So I’d implement “refuse to connect; drop the weaker anchor or fallback”.

### Q5 — “Propagate homogeneity to leaf nodes of the inferior node”
This is the trickiest part. What exactly are “leaf nodes of the inferior node”?
- In your skeleton, leaves connect to either `head` or `tail_root`. fileciteturn12file0
- So do you mean:
  - if tail_root switches id, then also switch {left_hind_paw,right_hind_paw,tail_tip} that are assigned to that same mouse (and similarly for head’s leaves)?

And how do we decide contradiction count?
- count leaves whose `exclusive` disagrees with proposed id?
- treat `(1,1)` or non-exclusive as “no contradiction”?

I can do this, but I need your exact rule for “contradiction”.

---

## Suggestions to simplify while matching your intent
You’re proposing a fairly complex “local re-optimization” over pairings + propagation. It can be implemented, but there’s a simpler variant that likely fixes your frame-198 problem immediately:

### Suggestion S1 — Anchor pairing should be selected by segmentation-first, not continuity-first
When both head candidates exist and are exclusive (0 vs 1), and both tail_root candidates exist, pick the anchor assignment that matches exclusives. Only use continuity as a tie-breaker.

This prevents choosing the mixed tail_root candidate `(vote_sum=(1,1), exclusive=None)` for an anchor if an exclusive candidate exists.

This is extremely aligned with your “color dominates” goal, and it avoids complex “try_other both directions”.

### Suggestion S2 — Add a hard “head–tail_root plausibility gate” before B4
After anchors are assigned, check head–tail_root distance for each mouse. If it fails:
- drop the **non-exclusive** anchor first (or fallback to prev anchor)
- rebuild mouse model (centroid/axis)
- then run B4.

This directly blocks the chimera without needing to steal anchors across mice.

Your proposed try_other is basically a more aggressive version of this. I’m fine implementing the aggressive version — I just want you to confirm you really need it.
