Assistant: You’re reading it correctly.

### Why `tol=13.216` happened (and why it’s too loose for your goal)

In your B7 rigid fallback, `tol` is computed as:

- `tol = min(graph_rigid_tol_mm_max, graph_rigid_tol_ratio * L)`  
- where **`L` is the template edge length (mm)** for that leaf’s incident edge (e.g., `head–right_ear_tip`).  

So with `graph_rigid_tol_ratio = 1.0` and **no tight absolute cap**, you effectively allow up to **~one full edge-length** of deviation (≈13 mm for the ear tip). That is exactly why you see:

- frame 962: `L=13.216` → `tol=13.216`
- frame 966: `L=13.216` → `tol=13.216`

That’s overly permissive for “agree-with-model” as you intend it (you want “a couple mm”, not “an entire ear-length”). The correct interpretation is: yes, `L` is the edge length; and yes, that tolerance makes it possible for a leaf that *just failed* the edge ratio check to still be re-attached via rigid-fit.

To match your intent, you want an **absolute cap** like `5 mm` (or even tighter) and keep ratio scaling only as a secondary limiter.

Your code already supports exactly that: `tol = min(mm_max, ratio*L)`. fileciteturn44file2  
So setting `graph_rigid_tol_mm_max=5` makes the ear tip require `err <= 5mm`, regardless of `L`. fileciteturn44file2

### Why “edge violation → rigid_assign back to same mouse” occurs

Example frame 966:

- edge check fails for mouse0 ear: `ratio=1.864` (too large; i.e. **D too small**).
- then rigid-fit says: `err0=7.163`, `err1=220.273`, `tol=13.216`  
- since `7.163 <= 13.216`, it reassigns to mouse0.

That’s internally consistent with the current logic: **rigid-fit is treated as a stronger global consistency cue than a single edge check**, because the edge violation might be due to noise in *either* node (head or ear) and the rigid fit uses many points.

But with your intended “conservative” rigid fallback, you should not allow `7.2 mm` to pass for an ear. Tightening the cap fixes this exact failure mode.

### Why you still get “head connected to other tail_root” (half-stitch / chimera)

Yes — continuity across frames is a plausible mechanism, especially when:

- tail_root has **ambiguous votes** (e.g. `(1,1)` → `exclusive=None`)
- head is strongly exclusive, but tail_root is not
- swap_state is `UNKNOWN` (or anchors are partially missing), so the model-building can fall back to previous anchors
- then B4’s centroid/axis terms can pull “the back half” to the wrong mouse because the axis is built from a mismatched head/tail_root pair.

The net effect is exactly what you describe: *front half of mouse0 + back half of mouse1*.

### Your “apply B7 to head–tail_root too” request: implement it **before** B4 (B3.5)

Doing it as a “B7-style” check *after* B6 is too late: by then, a wrong head–tail_root pairing already contaminated centroid/axis and therefore B4 assignments.

So the right place is **between B3 and B4**:

**B3.5**: check the template edge `head–tail_root` for each mouse; if either fails, try the alternative pairings:

- swap tails (try other tail_root for head)
- swap heads (try other head for tail_root)
- evaluate the 4 combinations (head_perm × tail_perm)

Then select the best combination with this priority:

1) maximize number of mice passing the edge ratio test  
2) minimize anchor exclusive penalties (seg-consistency)  
3) minimize vote contradictions  
4) minimize movement from the existing anchor assignment (conservative tie-break)

This targets your exact failure case (frame ~198→199) without dropping large parts of the animal.

### CLI correctness

Your `run_mouse3d.py` should accept:

- `--debug-stageB` / `--debug_stageB` fileciteturn44file4  
- `--graph_model` / `--graph-model` fileciteturn44file4  

(I also added an explicit `--frame` alias to the `--frames` argument so your existing habits won’t break.)

Here are the updated files (includes B3.5 head–tail_root pairing sanity + cached graph loading + strict rigid tolerance cap behavior):

- [Download mouse3d_patch_B35.zip](sandbox:/mnt/data/mouse3d_patch_B35.zip)

Drop `stage_b.py` into your `mice3d/` package and `run_mouse3d.py` into your `python/` folder (where you run it from).

After that, rerun around the transition you mentioned, e.g. frames 195–200, with `--debug-stageB --graph_model ...` and you should see new lines like:

- `B3.5 combo head_perm=... tail_perm=... ok_cnt=...`
- `B3.5 apply head_perm=... tail_perm=...`

Those are the moments where the code prevents the “front/back stitched across mice” state.

Assistant: The edge-length test is behaving *correctly* here — it’s just not testing (or fixing) the thing that actually creates the chimera in frame 997.

### What actually happens in frame 997
1) **B3 and B3.5 are fine.**  
You have clean anchor exclusives (`head: 0/1`, `tail_root: 0/1`), and B3.5 explicitly confirms both mice pass the **head–tail_root** edge check:

- `B3.5 ... ok_cnt=2 ... m0(D=52.321 ... ok=True) m1(D=50.572 ... ok=True)`  
So the *anchor pairing* after B3.5 is coherent.

2) **Then B4 *overrides the anchor identity for tail_root*.**  
This is the real source of the chimera.

Look at the tail_root assignment in B4:

- For tail_root item **A** (exclusive=0, P=(347.852,…))  
  `assign_mouse=0 cost=798` (huge)  
  `assign_mouse=1 cost=525` (still huge but smaller)

- For tail_root item **B** (exclusive=1, P=(191.967,…))  
  `assign_mouse=0 cost=128`  
  `assign_mouse=1 cost=1997`

So the minimum total is achieved by **swapping tail_root**:
- `B4 result bodypart=tail_root :: A.mouse_id=1 B.mouse_id=0`

That means after B4, mouse0 has head at ~328 (correct) but tail_root at ~191 (belongs to the other mouse) → **front/back chimera**.

Why did B4 do that? Because the continuity term is astronomically large here and dominates everything (exclusive penalty is only 9). Your `tail_root` continuity predictions are bad this frame:

- In the SWAP debug you can see `T0=(493.058, 62.307, -12.672)` for tail_root mouse0, which is far away from the “correct” tail_root candidate at 347.852…  
That’s exactly how you get a continuity term like ~798: the tracker thinks tail_root should be somewhere else (likely due to a spurious tail_root in a previous frame).

3) **B7 leaf edge check *does* detect the consequence.**  
It flags:

- `m=1 left_hind_paw–tail_root D=167.956 ratio=0.158 ok=False`

That huge distance is a *symptom* of the swapped tail_root in B4.

Then B7 does the right thing for what it is allowed to do:
- It **frees left_hind_paw** from mouse1 and reassigns it to mouse0 where the edge is plausible:
  - `try_other ... D=24.955 ratio=1.064 ok=True`
  - `reassigned leaf=left_hind_paw -> m=0`

So B7 is working: it cleans up the leaf that became inconsistent due to the bad anchor identity. But…

### Why it can’t “fix the chimera”
Because B7 (as currently configured/logged) is **leaf-only** and doesn’t modify `head` or `tail_root`. In your B7 log, only leaves are checked and modified — tail_root is never touched. So the swapped tail_root identity survives, even though B7 repaired one hind paw.

That’s why you see “chimera re-occurs” and also see B7 apparently doing something sensible at the same time.

---

## What to do about it (conceptually)
The minimal, targeted fix is:

**Do not allow B4 to swap anchor parts (`head`, `tail_root`) away from the B3.5 anchor pairing.**

You already did the hard work in B3.5 to ensure head–tail_root is consistent. But then B4 reassigns tail_root like any other bodypart. That defeats the point of B3.5.

There are a few ways to enforce this; the cleanest is:

- After B3.5 chooses the anchor pairing, **lock** `mouse_id` for the anchor items (head, tail_root) accordingly and skip the “assign2” optimization for those bodyparts in B4.

This keeps continuity from “inventing” a swapped tail_root that creates chimeras, while still letting B4/B7 handle all other bodyparts normally.

A softer alternative is to add an extra cost term in B4 for anchor parts that penalizes violating the B3.5 pairing (but locking is simpler and more deterministic).

---

## Why the rigid-fit RMS is huge in B7 (69mm / 63mm)
That’s another hint that the global configuration is inconsistent after B4. Once anchor identity is wrong, the set of points per mouse is no longer close to a rigid template, so Kabsch fitting reports a large RMS. It’s not the cause; it’s a diagnostic symptom.

---

If you want, I can implement the “lock anchors after B3.5” change in your uploaded `stage_b.py` (surgical patch, no other logic changes). That should eliminate exactly this frame-997 type chimera, because the trigger is B4 swapping tail_root against your validated anchor pairing.

User: Yes, please implement the "lock anchors after B3.5".  

And please comment on the value of B4 after all. Does it do any good? (Do not change anything. Only comment.)

User: I notice you are having problems with code versions. The uploaded file is exactly the file you provided for downloading. It works properly ( as mentioned), and it is the version I asked you to change.

Assistant: You’re seeing the “chimera” in frame 997 because the **anchor pairing is fixed in B3/B3.5**, but then **B4 is still allowed to re-assign the anchor parts (“head”, “tail_root”)** based on the generic cost terms (continuity/centroid/axis/exclusive). Once **tail_root** flips in B4, your leaf-only B7 can’t reliably undo the *front/back* splice, because B7 (by design) is operating on leaf edges, while **tail_root is not a leaf**. This is consistent with how `anchor_parts=("head","tail_root")` and `graph_leaf_only=True` are used. fileciteturn47file0

## Implemented: lock anchors after B3.5
I implemented a small **B3.6** step inside `stage_b_assign()`:

1. **After B3.5**, it assigns `mouse_id` for the *anchor items* (head, tail_root) to match the pairing implied by `anchor_assigned`.
2. Then **B4 skips those anchor bodyparts** entirely (`head` and `tail_root`), so they cannot be swapped back by continuity/axis/centroid terms.

This directly targets the failure mode you saw at frame 997: B3.5 “keep” is correct, but B4 later flips tail_root anyway.

### Patch file
Download and drop-in replace your existing `stage_b.py` with this patched file:

[Download stage_b_locked.py](sandbox:/mnt/data/stage_b_locked.py)

(Just rename it to `stage_b.py` in your package/module location.)

## Why B4 still matters (comment only)
B4 is still doing important work for **non-anchor bodyparts**:

- It’s the main place where you turn “two competing 3D hypotheses” into a **consistent per-mouse assignment**, using a combination of **exclusive seg evidence + continuity + centroid proximity + axis proximity** (see `assignment_cost()` and the “B4 assign2 …” logic). fileciteturn47file0  
- Without B4, you’d largely fall back to “whatever was triangulated / DLC id structure”, and you’d lose much of the cross-camera / temporal coherence that your pipeline is achieving.

Where B4 is *not* helpful is exactly what you observed: if you let it act on **anchors**, it can override the anchor pairing decided earlier (B3/B3.5) and reintroduce half-stitch chimeras. The lock fixes that by restricting B4 to the parts where it’s actually beneficial.

If you want, paste the new debug lines you’ll get around frame 997 (you should see `B3.6 lock_anchor ...` and `B4 skip bodypart=... (locked anchor)`), and we can confirm it’s behaving exactly as intended.

Assistant: This log looks **exactly like the “lock anchors after B3.5” fix is working**.

### What changed vs the broken frame-997 behavior you showed earlier
Previously, the chimera happened because:

- B3/B3.5 picked a good anchor pairing (head↔tail_root edge OK),
- **but then B4 re-assigned `tail_root`** (because its continuity term went crazy), which produced the “front/back splice”.

Now you have these two key lines:

- `B3.6 lock_anchor part=head :: ...`
- `B3.6 lock_anchor part=tail_root :: ...`

followed by:

- `B4 skip bodypart=head (locked anchor)`
- `B4 skip bodypart=tail_root (locked anchor)`

So in frame 997, **head and tail_root cannot be swapped anymore by B4**, which is precisely the failure mechanism that caused the chimera.

### Why the edge-length checks “look fine” now
Because the anchors remain consistent:

- B3.5 `head–tail_root` passes for both mice:  
  `m0(D=52.321, ratio=1.003)` and `m1(D=50.572, ratio=1.038)` → both OK.

And in B7 (leaf-only), all leaf edges are also OK, *including hind paws vs tail_root*, which would have been the first thing to explode if tail_root got swapped.

Notably, there is **no `B7 free leaf=...`** and no reassignment needed—another strong sign the chimera is gone.

### What about that “continuity insanity” you saw before?
You can still see hints of it in the SWAP diagnostics for tail_root (big costs), but now it **can’t damage identity coherence** because tail_root is locked and will update the state with the correct anchor afterward. In practice, this should make subsequent frames *more* stable.

### Your question: does the edge-length test “not work correctly”?
In this frame 997 log: it **does** work correctly. It’s just that the real chimera source was **not the leaf edges**—it was **B4 swapping the anchor part `tail_root`**, which the edge test never touched (because B7 is leaf-only). After anchor locking, the edge test has nothing dramatic to fix here.

## Comment on B4’s value (no changes, just assessment)
B4 is still doing useful work for **non-anchor bodyparts**:

- It resolves “two plausible 3D candidates” for a bodypart into a consistent mouse assignment, using a blend of segmentation exclusives, continuity, centroid, and axis proximity.
- In your log, B4 is making strong, sensible assignments (often aligning with exclusives) for ears/paws/nose.

The problematic behavior wasn’t “B4 in general”—it was “B4 being allowed to re-decide anchor identities,” because that can create a chimera even if everything else is fine. Locking anchors turns B4 back into what it’s good at: cleaning the *non-anchor* parts.

If you still *visually* see a chimera in frame 997 after this patch, the next thing I’d check is the downstream visualization/CSV interpretation (e.g., mixing `mouse_id` from different outputs), because this Stage-B trace shows coherent anchors and coherent skeleton edges for that frame.

Assistant: ### What’s going wrong in frame 995 (and why it *looks* like “two rays intersect fine”)

Your intuition is right: this is essentially a **2-ray triangulation** that is geometrically “self-consistent” (small ray–ray closest distance + acceptable reprojection), but **semantically wrong** because the two 2D points come from *different* physical mice due to cross-camera DLC-ID inconsistency.

Concretely in your `cleaningdebug995.out`:

**Stage A produces two tail_root hypotheses** (because it tries to find up to two disjoint 3D points per bodypart to represent two mice):

- **CHOSEN#0 (good)**: `tail_root` **pair** using cams `[0,2]` with `dlc_ids=[0,0]`, low error  
  `P≈(179.820,162.765,16.834)` with `rms_px=0.593`

- **CHOSEN#1 (suspicious / phantom)**: `tail_root` **pair** using cams `[1,2]` with `dlc_ids=[1,1]`, much worse error  
  `P≈(188.949,89.430,38.133)` with `rms_px=4.617`

This “phantom” CHOSEN#1 passes Stage A gates because they are *purely geometric*:
- ray closest approach for that pair is small enough (`d=6.836mm < 20mm`)
- reprojection still under the threshold (`max≈5.9px < 12px`)
- bbox gate is permissive (margin is huge)

That’s totally possible even when the two rays originate from **different mice**. If the mice are in a compact region and DLC points are noisy / swapped, “cross-mouse” rays can still pass close in space and look valid.

---

### Why Stage B doesn’t reliably kill it (today)

Stage B *does* notice something is fishy — you can see it in **B1 segmentation voting** for the phantom tail_root pair:

- `tail_root kind=pair` with obs `(cam2_front dlc_id=1)` votes `(0,1)` (mouse1 color)
- and `(cam3_side dlc_id=1)` votes `(1,0)` (mouse0 color)

So the item becomes:
- `vote_sum=(1,1)` and `exclusive=None`

That’s exactly the signature of “this 3D point was built from conflicting mouse colors across cams”.

But: because tail_root is an **anchor**, and because B7 currently focuses on **leaf edges only**, this ambiguous anchor can still survive long enough to create a front/back mix (chimera), especially in frames where one mouse lacks a clean tail_root hypothesis and B3.5 ends up picking a “best available” configuration where one mouse’s head–tail distance is bad (you can already see B3.5 reporting a failing ratio for one mouse in frame 995).

---

### Your proposed fix makes sense: enable head–tail_root edge checking when an anchor is “effectively a leaf”

What you want is:

> If a mouse has head + tail_root assigned, but **tail_root has no other present neighbors** (hind paws / tail_tip missing), then tail_root is *effectively* a leaf. In that case, a bad head–tail_root length is a strong reason to **drop/loosen that anchor**, just like B7 does for leaf nodes.

That would directly address the “phantom second tail_root” pattern:
- it typically has **worse RMS**
- it often has **mixed seg votes** (exclusive=None)
- and it often becomes the only available tail_root for a mouse when the true one is occluded → making it “effectively leaf-like”.

Even with your global ratio band (0.667–1.5), this helps in cases like frame 995 where the bad head–tail_root distance is wildly off; and if it isn’t wildly off, the “effective-leaf” rule still gives you permission to drop a weak anchor when it’s unsupported by any other connected points.

---

### Two quick questions before coding (to avoid surprises)

1) **When head–tail_root fails and the “effective leaf” endpoint is tail_root**, do you want to:
   - **A)** drop only that tail_root 3D item (unassign / ignore it), leaving the mouse without a tail_root this frame, or
   - **B)** also **free all leaves that depend on tail_root** (hind paws, tail_tip) because the anchor for their edge checks vanished?

   (A is minimal; B is more consistent with the current B7 logic of “no_neighbor → free”.)

2) If the dropped tail_root was the **only** tail_root candidate for that mouse, should we attempt a **try_other** swap against the other mouse’s tail_root **only if** the other mouse’s head–tail_root edge is also bad (i.e., both anchors can’t be right)?

   (This keeps it conservative and avoids identity flips driven by geometry alone.)

---

### Why this is fundamentally “Stage A’s fault” (but fixable in Stage B)

Stage A is doing the right thing *geometrically*, but it has no semantic notion of “these two rays came from the same animal”. With inconsistent per-camera DLC IDs, that assumption is violated, and **2-view triangulation is especially vulnerable**: it’s much easier for two wrong rays to still intersect nicely than for three.

So yes: implementing your “effective-leaf head–tail_root length check” in B7 (or immediately after B3.5) is a reasonable *Stage B* safety valve that specifically targets this failure mode, without needing to redesign Stage A.

If you answer the two questions above, I can translate it into a precise B7 rule that matches your current code style (and keeps the “don’t throw away existing information unless we must” philosophy).

Assistant: ### What’s going wrong in frame 995 (and why it *looks* like a valid 2-ray triangulation)

From your `cleaningdebug995.out`, Stage A produces **two** `tail_root` 3D candidates:

- **CHOSEN#0** (good): `cams=[0,2] dlc_ids=[0,0]` with low `rms_px=0.593`
- **CHOSEN#1** (bad/phantom): `cams=[1,2] dlc_ids=[1,1]` with **high** but still “accepted” `rms_px=4.617`

The key indicator that **CHOSEN#1 is cross-mouse** is already visible in Stage B’s B1 segmentation votes for the exact 2D observations used by that triangulation:

- `cam2_front dlc_id=1 uv=(460.688,476.673)` votes **mouse1** `(0,1)`
- `cam3_side  dlc_id=1 uv=(571.954,448.406)` votes **mouse0** `(1,0)`

So Stage A is triangulating a 3D point from **one tail_root observation on mouse1** + **another on mouse0**.

Why can this still produce a “plausible” triangulation?
- With **two views**, you can *always* compute a closest-point solution between two rays. If the two selected 2D points happen to be close to each other’s epipolar geometry (even accidentally), the optimizer can find a 3D point with reprojection error below your acceptance threshold.  
- That point can be **nowhere near either physical mouse** (your “non-existent point in 3D space”), yet still have acceptable `rms_px`, especially if `--accept-rms-pair` is permissive.

### Why B7 (as it existed) often can’t catch this
Your B7 checks were **leaf-only**, and `tail_root` is not a leaf. Normally, the *damage* from a wrong tail_root is detected indirectly because connected leaves (hind paws / tail tip) create edge-length violations.

But in the specific failure mode you describe, **tail_root becomes “factually a leaf”** because the mouse is missing its tail-tip / hind-paw nodes (occlusion, DLC failure, filtering, etc.). Then:
- there are **no tail_root-adjacent leaf edges available** to flag the error,
- so the bogus tail_root can survive.

That matches your observation: “tail_root does not have any leaf points… it would be completely okay to delete the edge”.

---

## Implemented change: B7.2 “effective-leaf tail_root” head↔tail_root edge check + drop only that tail_root

Per your refs:
- **Drop only that tail_root**, do not try the other (B3.5 already explored anchor permutations).
- Trigger only when `tail_root` is **effectively isolated** (no other neighbors besides `head` are present for that mouse).
- Use the same global ratio band (`graph_min_ratio`, `graph_max_ratio`).

### What it does
After B7 runs, B7.2:
1. checks, per mouse, whether `tail_root` has *any* non-head neighbor present (among the graph neighbors that exist in your tracked bodyparts, typically `tail_tip`, `left_hind_paw`, `right_hind_paw`).
2. If none exist → `tail_root` is treated as **effectively a leaf**.
3. Then it tests the **head–tail_root** edge ratio.
4. If it fails → it **drops that tail_root** (sets its `mouse_id=None`) and also clears `anchor_assigned["tail_root"][m]` so it **does not seed continuity**.
5. If any tail_root was dropped, it **rebuilds the mouse model** from anchors so the next-frame state is consistent.

You’ll see new debug lines like:
- `B7.2 head-tail_root m=... effective_leaf=True ... ok=False ...`
- `B7.2 drop tail_root m=... reason=head-tail_root_violation`

## Why “lock anchors after B3.5” matters for the frame 997 chimera

In your earlier frame 997 log **before locking**, `tail_root` got reassigned in B4 despite strong segmentation, because the **continuity term** can dominate (you had `cont` terms in the hundreds/thousands for tail_root in that frame). Since `tail_root` isn’t checked as a leaf in B7, the wrong anchor can persist and create the “front/back swap chimera”.

With **B3.6 anchor locking**, B4 cannot flip head/tail_root anymore, and B7 leaf checks behave as intended. So the remaining pathology you see is consistent with **Stage A constructing a cross-mouse pairwise triangulation** that slips through on RMS.

---

## Your question about B4 (comment only, no code change)

B4 still does real work and is not redundant:

- It’s the **main resolver** for most bodyparts when segmentation isn’t exclusive (mixed votes, `(1,1)`), or when segmentation is locally misleading (occlusion cases you described).
- It injects **continuity + centroid/axis geometry** to stabilize assignments across frames even when per-frame segmentation votes are weak or contradictory across cameras.
- It also indirectly affects **B5 cam_map** (because it sets `mouse_id` for many 3D items, which then votes per-camera DLC id mapping used for singles).

With anchor locking + B7, B4’s role is basically: “solve everything that’s not an anchor using a geometry + continuity cost, with segmentation acting as a strong prior when exclusive.”

[DEBUG stageB] frame=995 :: MODEL(B3.5) mouse=1 swap_state=NOSWAP head=(217.06801470463444, 155.24482742775353, 58.44224681272061) tail_root=(179.81957322307903, 162.76512477526046, 16.833628926335088) centroid=(198.44379396385673, 159.004976101507, 37.637937869527846) valid_axis=True
[DEBUG stageB] frame=995 :: END swap_state=NOSWAP swap_age=0

Assistant: Yes — that log is consistent with the rules you’ve enabled, and the **B7.2 tail_root drop is doing exactly what it was designed to do** for this “phantom tail_root from cross-mouse rays” case.

Here’s how to read it:

## 1) The three “B7 drop leaf=…” lines
These are **plain B7 (leaf-only)** decisions:

- `left_hind_paw`, `right_hind_paw`, and `nose_tip` each failed their **own** edge check for their current mouse, then could **not** be rescued by:
  - “try_other” (edge check against the other mouse’s neighbor), or
  - rigid fallback (because you tightened `tol=5mm` and the error was still too large)

So B7 drops them (“failed edge and rigid”). That’s exactly what the current B7 policy means: *if a leaf node is geometrically implausible relative to its neighbor and can’t be plausibly moved, discard that 3D point*.

In this particular frame, it’s very likely those hind-paw failures are a downstream symptom of the **wrong tail_root** (or missing good tail_root) making the hind-paw-to-tail_root distances crazy. The nose-tip failure is independent (nose is checked against head), so it can also be genuinely wrong due to occlusion/cross-ray.

## 2) Why `effective_leaf=True` for tail_root
This line:

`B7.2 head-tail_root m=0 effective_leaf=True other_nbrs=['left_hind_paw','right_hind_paw','tail_tip'] ...`

means: for mouse 0, tail_root’s other neighbors (hind paws, tail tip) are *not present as usable 3D points anymore* **at the moment B7.2 is evaluated**.

And that’s true in your log because **B7 just dropped left/right hind paw**, and tail_tip wasn’t available for that mouse.

So tail_root is “factually isolated” → B7.2 considers it “leaf-like”.

## 3) The head↔tail_root test is massively violated (so dropping tail_root is correct)
`L=52.484 D=157.612 ratio=0.333`

That’s far outside your global band `[0.667, 1.5]`. This is the hallmark of the phantom: the tail_root candidate is simply not on the same animal as the head.

Therefore:

`B7.2 drop tail_root m=0 reason=head-tail_root_violation`

✅ This is exactly the intended behavior for the case you described (“front view tail_root of one mouse matched with side view tail_root of the other → phantom 3D point”).

## 4) Why the later `MODEL(B3.5)` print still shows a tail_root for mouse 0
This is important: it **does not mean the dropped 3D tail_root survived**.

After dropping, the model rebuild function falls back to the **previous frame’s anchor** when the current frame’s anchor is missing. That’s why mouse 0 still shows a plausible tail_root and `valid_axis=True` in the model printout.

So conceptually:
- current frame: tail_root for mouse0 is *removed from observations / won’t be saved as a 3D point / won’t update anchor continuity*
- but centroid/axis computation for the rest of the frame is allowed to use the last good anchor as a fallback, rather than leaving the axis undefined.

That fallback is intentional, and it’s generally stabilizing.

---

# Is this what it “should” do?
### Tail_root drop: **yes**
Your B7.2 decision is spot on: tail_root became effectively isolated and violates head↔tail_root strongly → drop it.

### Leaf drops: **also consistent with current B7 policy**
Whether you *want* those drops is a separate question. With the strict `tol=5mm`, B7 will be aggressive when a leaf edge violates and can’t be rescued.

If your goal for the frame-995 case is “**drop only the phantom tail_root and keep the rest if possible**”, the *mechanism* is slightly mis-ordered: B7 drops the hind paws first (because tail_root is still wrong), which then makes tail_root “effectively leaf” and triggers B7.2.

That ordering is logically consistent, but it can discard more points than necessary in frames where the **root cause is a bad tail_root**.

If you want, the next refinement would be to make B7.2 *preemptive* in exactly this situation (detect huge head↔tail_root violation early, drop tail_root first), so hind paws become `no_neighbor` instead of “violation”, and are much more likely to be kept. I won’t change anything unless you ask, but that’s the lever if you want “drop only tail_root” behavior more often.

For now: your log shows the feature working as designed, and the tail_root phantom is being eliminated.
